Clarifications on Key Terms and Scope
Commentator clarifies ambiguous terms and scope conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's clarifications regarding the meaning of 'must', the scope of 'kalam', and the inclusion of specific topics in the universal matters station.

Core Idea
Precise definitions prevent misunderstanding of the book's structure.

The author's commentator provides essential clarifications to avoid misinterpretations. He explains that 'must' means commendatory, not logical; that 'science of kalam' includes both its parts and prerequisites; and that the second station, though called 'universal matters', can touch on specific topics by digression. These fine points ensure the reader correctly grasps the author's intent.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary section
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'قوله: (ما يذكر فيه...' which contains three clarifications.
2
Understand the clarification on 'must'
The commentator says 'must' here means commendatory obligation (wujub istihsani), not logical or religious necessity. This softens the force of the word.
3
Understand the scope of 'kalam'
The commentator clarifies that 'science of kalam' includes both its core subjects and its prerequisites, like the eight introductory points.
This ensures we don't exclude important introductory material.
4
Understand the second station's scope
Although the second station is about universal matters, it may include specific topics like 'necessity' and 'priority' by digression. This clarifies apparent inconsistencies.

The Six Stations of al-Mawaqif and Their Rationale
The author enumerates the six mawaqif and explains their sequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six mawaqif (stations) of al-Mawaqif and the logical reasons for their sequence.

Core Idea
The six stations follow a logical order based on dependency and evidential priority.

The author of al-Mawaqif organizes his book around six major stations. In this passage, he explains what each station covers and why they appear in this specific order. The arrangement is not arbitrary; it reflects the logical dependencies between topics in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall picture of the six stations and their sequence.
2
Identify the six stations
Note each station's subject matter: preliminaries, universal matters, accidents, substances, divine matters, and scriptural matters. See how they are defined by distinctions.
The first station is about what must be presented first; the rest are divided by scope and subject.
3
Understand the rationale for the order
Look for why preliminaries come first, then universal matters, then accidents before substances, etc. The passage gives reasons of dependence and evidence.
Accidents are placed before substances because they are used as evidence for substances.
4
Consider the alternative view
Note that some scholars reverse the order of accidents and substances because the existence of accidents depends on substances. The author acknowledges this alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكتاب مرتب على ستة مواقف ) وذلك لأن ما يذكر فيه إما أن يجب تقديمه في علم الكلام، وهو الموقف الأول في المقدمات أو لا يجب وحينئذ إما أن يبحث فيه عما لا يختص بواحد من الأقسام الثلاثة للموجود ، وهو الموقف الثاني في الأمور العامة، أو عما يختص فإما بالممكن الذي لا يقوم بنفسه بل بغيره، وهو الموقف الثالث في الأعراض، أو بالممكن الذي يقوم بنفسه وهو الموقف الرابع في الجواهر، وإما بالواجب تعالى فإما باعتبار إرساله الرسل وبعثه الأنبياء وهو الموقف السادس في السمعيات، أو لا باعتباره وهو الموقف الخامس في الإلهيات والوجه في التقديم والتأخير أن المقدمات يجب تقديمها على الكل، والأمور العامة كالمبادئ لما عداها، والسمعيات متوقفة على الإلهيات المتوقفة على مباحث الممكنات، وأما تقديم العرض على الجوهر فلأنه قد يستدل بأحوال الأعراض على أحوال الجواهر، كما يستدل بأحوال الحركة والسكون على حدوث الأجسام، وبقطع المسافة المتناهية في زمان متناه على عدم تركبها من الجواهر الأفراد التي لا تتناهى، ومنهم من قدم مباحث الجوهر نظراً إلى أن وجود العرض متوقف على وجوده .
And the book is arranged according to six stations (mawāqif). That is because what is mentioned in it is either what must be presented in the science of kalām, which is the first station on the preliminaries, or not. Then either it investigates what is not specific to any of the three divisions of the existent, which is the second station on universal matters, or what is specific. Then either it concerns the contingent that does not subsist by itself but by another, which is the third station on accidents, or the contingent that subsists by itself, which is the fourth station on substances. And either it concerns the Necessary, exalted, either with respect to His sending messengers and raising prophets, which is the sixth station on scriptural matters, or not with that respect, which is the fifth station on divine matters. The reason for the order and delay is that the preliminaries must be presented over everything, and the universal matters are like principles for what comes after them, and the scriptural matters depend on the divine matters which depend on the investigations of contingents. As for presenting the accident before the substance, it is because one may infer from the states of accidents to the states of substances, just as one infers from the states of motion and rest to the temporal origination of bodies, and from crossing a finite distance in a finite time to their not being composed of indivisible atoms that are infinite. And some have presented the investigations of substance, considering that the existence of the accident depends on its existence.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage lays out the overall structure of the book into six mawaqif (stations) and justifies their arrangement. It begins by stating that the book is organized into six stations. The reason for this is that the material covered either must be placed at the beginning (the first station on preliminaries) or not. If not, then it either deals with topics that are not specific to any of the three categories of existence (the second station on universal matters) or specifically deals with one of them: either the contingent that does not subsist by itself (accidents, third station) or the contingent that subsists by itself (substances, fourth station). Or it deals with the Necessary Being (God), either with reference to sending messengers and prophets (sixth station on scriptural matters) or without that reference (fifth station on divine matters). The passage then explains the logic of the order: preliminaries come first, universal matters are like principles for the rest, scriptural matters depend on divine matters which themselves depend on investigations of contingents. The accident is placed before the substance because accidents are used as evidence for substances (e.g., motion and rest indicate temporal origination of bodies, and traversing finite distance in finite time shows bodies are not composed of infinite indivisible atoms). However, some scholars reversed this order because the existence of an accident depends on the substance. The passage thus provides both the author's preferred order and an alternative.

Key Terms
مواقف
مقدمات
الأمور العامة
الأعراض
الجواهر
الإلهيات
السمعيات
التمسك
الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The order of the six stations is arbitrary or based on convention.
Correction: The order is based on logical dependencies: preliminaries precede everything, universal matters are principles, scriptural matters depend on divine matters, and accidents are placed before substances because they provide evidence for substances.

Checkpoint
Why is the accident (al-‘araḍ) presented before the substance (al-jawhar) in the arrangement of the mawaqif?
A) Because accidents are more fundamental than substances.
B) Because one can infer from the states of accidents to the states of substances, e.g., motion and rest indicate temporal origination.
C) Because the author considers accidents to be the only real existents.
D) Because the accident is the cause of the substance.

Key Takeaway
The six mawaqif of al-Mawaqif are arranged according to a logical hierarchy of dependence and evidence, making the book's structure a reflection of kalām's inner logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you approach your own learning of Islamic theology with a sense of what must come first, as the author insists on ordering by dependency, or do you jump into topics without foundation? What would it cost you to adopt a more disciplined sequence in your studies?
?
The author prioritizes rational arguments (ilahiyat) over scriptural arguments (sam'iyat) by placing them earlier. In your daily faith, do you sometimes rely solely on verses and hadith without first grounding yourself in rational proofs? How might this imbalance affect the strength of your convictions?
?
Accidents are studied before substances because they provide evidence for deeper realities. In your life, do you pay attention to the temporary signs and events ('accidents') that point to spiritual truths, or do you overlook them as insignificant? What is one 'accident' you have experienced recently that could lead you to a deeper understanding of Allah?

Method of Learning

Refining 'What is Mentioned in It' and the Obligation
Clarification of the terms 'ma yudhkaru fihi', 'wujub', and 'taqdim'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meanings of the key terms in the author's phrase, especially the customary nature of the obligation to present the topic of kalam first.

Core Idea
Obligation here is not religious duty but pedagogical convention.

This lesson unpacks the commentator's clarification of two critical terms: 'what is mentioned in it' and 'obligation'. The goal is to avoid common misunderstandings that would apply too broadly or too strictly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first clarifying 'what is mentioned in it', then explaining 'obligation' and 'presentation'.
The passage has two sub-parts; focus on the first part here.
2
Identify the intended meaning of 'what is mentioned in it'
The commentator says it means the intended subject matter, not the introductory sermon. Why exclude the sermon? Because the sermon is not the topic itself.
3
Understand the nature of the obligation and presentation
The obligation is customary (istiḥsānī), not logical or legal. Presentation means priority over everything else, so it excludes investigations that are only principles for some topics.
This prevents overextending the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ما يذكر فيه أي المقصود الذي يذكر فيه فلا يرد الخطبة، والمراد بالوجوب الوجوب الاستحساني وبالتقديم التقديم على كل ما عداه فلا يرد بعض المباحث الذي هو كالمبادي لبعض دون بعض، كالأمور العامة . قوله : ( في علم الكلام أي في تحصيله سواء كان جزءاً منه كمباحث النظر، أو لا كالرؤوس الثمانية التي هي مبادئ الشروع .
His statement: 'What is mentioned in it' means the intended subject matter that is mentioned therein, so the sermon (khuṭba) is not included. And by 'obligation' (wujūb) is meant the customary obligation (al-wujūb al-istiḥsānī), and by 'presentation' (taqdīm) is meant presenting it over everything else, so that certain investigations that are like principles for some but not others, such as universal matters, are not included. His statement: 'in the science of kalām' means in acquiring it, whether it is part of it, like the investigations of logical reasoning (al-naẓar), or not, like the eight points that are the preliminaries of beginning.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two parts of the author's text. First, it explains the phrase 'ma yudhkaru fihi' (what is mentioned in it) by stating that it refers to the intended subject matter, not the introductory sermon (khuṭba). Then it clarifies the key terms: 'wujūb' (obligation) is meant in the sense of customary or commendable obligation (istiḥsānī), not intellectual or legal necessity. 'Taqdīm' (presentation) means priority over everything else, so it does not cover investigations that are only principles for some topics (like universal matters). Second, it explains the phrase 'fī ‘ilm al-kalām' (in the science of kalām) as referring to the acquisition of that science, whether the topic is part of kalām (like investigations of logical reasoning, naẓar) or not part of it but still necessary for beginning (like the eight points that are the preliminaries for starting). This lesson thus refines the understanding of the scope and nature of the 'obligatory' presentation of topics.

الوجوب الاستحساني
التقديم
الخطبة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'wujūb al-taqdīm' implies a logical or religious necessity.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states it is customary (istiḥsānī), meaning commendable and based on pedagogical preference, not strict necessity.

Checkpoint
What is meant by 'al-wujūb al-istiḥsānī' in this passage?

C) Something that is commendable and customary, not strictly necessary.
D) Something that is obligatory only for certain people.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to present kalam first is a pedagogical convention, not a necessity.

The Scope of 'In the Science of Kalam'
Clarification of the phrase 'fi ilm al-kalam' and inclusion of preliminaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the phrase 'in the science of kalam' refers to acquiring its knowledge, including both its core investigations and its necessary preliminaries.

Core Idea
The study of kalam includes its principles, even if they are not part of the science itself.

The second part of the passage clarifies what it means to discuss topics 'in the science of kalam'. It explains that this includes both topics that are part of the science (like logical reasoning) and those that are merely preliminary (like the eight points). This prevents confusion about whether preliminaries belong to the science.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the phrase 'fī ‘ilm al-kalām' and how the commentator expands its meaning.
2
Distinguish between two types of content
The commentator says 'whether it is part of it, like investigations of logical reasoning, or not, like the eight points'. Identify what counts as part of the science versus what is preliminary.
Logical reasoning (naẓar) is integral; the eight points are external but necessary.
3
Understand the acquisition process
The phrase 'in the science of kalam' is interpreted as 'in acquiring it'. This shifts focus from the static subject matter to the dynamic process of learning.
Thus, even if a topic is not strictly within kalam, if it is needed to begin studying kalam, it is covered.

The Second Station: Universal Matters and Their Scope
Commentator defines second mawqif and defends its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second mawqif on universal matters is defined and why discussing attributes like pre-eternity does not violate its universality.

Core Idea
Universal matters include specific topics by digression; divine attributes are not substances or accidents.

In this passage, the commentator explains the second mawqif's scope. He addresses a potential confusion: if this station is about universal matters, why does it include topics like necessity (wujub) and priority (taqaddum) that are specific to one category? The commentator clarifies that such specific topics are mentioned only by way of digression. He also tackles the issue of divine attributes like pre-eternity (qidam), arguing that they are not specific in a way that contradicts the station's purpose because attributes are neither substances nor accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the commentator's main points.
Notice the technical terms: al-umur al-‘amma, al-istitrad, al-wujub, al-taqaddum, al-qidam.
2
Identify the potential contradiction
The objection is: if the second mawqif is about universal (non-specific) matters, why does it mention attributes like qidam which seem specific to the Necessary? Identify this tension in the passage.
The commentator uses the phrase 'لا ينافي' (does not contradict) to introduce his response.
3
Understand the commentator's first response
The commentator explains that attributes like qidam do not exist in substances or accidents, and the divine attributes are not other than the essence. Therefore, discussing them does not make the mawqif specific to one category.
Key phrase: 'فإن الصفات ليست منهما على أنها ليست غير الذات'.
4
Consider the alternative interpretations
The passage mentions two additional views: (1) essential pre-eternity does not exist in anything other than the Necessary at all; (2) non-specificity means not being specific along with its opposite. Note these as part of the broader discussion.
This shows the depth of kalam methodology.
5
Summarize the lesson
Recap: The second mawqif is about universal matters, but specific topics can enter by digression. Attributes are not problematic because they are not substances or accidents.
This reinforces the coherent structure of al-Mawaqif.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وهو الموقف الثاني في الأمور العامة) أي هو المقصود من الموقف الثاني وإن ذكر بالاستطراد في هذا الموقف ما يختص بواحد من الأقسام الثلاثة كالوجوب والتقدم، ووجود القدم الصفات لا ينافي القول باختصاصه بالواجب على معنى عدم وجوده في الجوهر، والعرض، فإن الصفات ليست منهما على أنها ليست غير الذات وأيضاً قالوا: فالقدم الذاتي لا يوجد فيه أصلاً ، وقيل : المراد بعدم الاختصاص أن لا يختص مع مقابله كما أشير إليه في أول هذا الموقف، كما سيجيء زيادة بحث إن شاء الله تعالى .
His statement: 'And it is the second station on universal matters' means it is the intended subject of the second station, although what is specific to one of the three categories is mentioned by way of digression in this station, such as necessity (al-wujūb) and priority (al-taqaddum). The existence of the attribute of pre-eternity (al-qidam) does not contradict the statement that it is specific to the Necessary in the sense that it does not exist in the substance and accident, for the attributes are not from these two categories, seeing that they are not other than the essence. Also they said: Essential pre-eternity does not exist in it at all. And it is said: What is meant by non-specificity is that it is not specific along with its opposite, as was indicated at the beginning of this station, and a further discussion will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's description of the second mawqif as 'al-umūr al-‘āmma' (universal matters). The commentator clarifies that this mawqif is intended to cover universal matters, but it also includes by way of digression some topics that are specific to one of the three categories (e.g., necessity and priority). The commentator then addresses a potential concern: how can attributes like pre-eternity (qidam) be discussed here when they are specific to the Necessary? He responds that the existence of such attributes does not negate the claim that they are specific to the Necessary in the sense that they do not exist in substances or accidents, because the divine attributes are not themselves substances or accidents—they are not other than the essence. He also mentions another view: that 'non-specificity' means the attribute is not exclusive to one category along with its opposite (e.g., existence is common while non-existence is its opposite). The commentator ends by noting that this will be discussed further later.

Correction: The commentator explains that attributes are included by digression and are not substances or accidents, so their discussion does not violate the mawqif's universality.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The second mawqif covers universal matters, but includes specific topics by digression; attributes like pre-eternity are not problematic because they are not substances or accidents.

The First Marsad: Customary Obligation and Absolute Topics
Explains the first marsad and its key notions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first marsad introduces a customary rather than intellectual obligation and what kind of topics must be presented in every science according to the commentator.

Core Idea
The obligation to present definition, subject, and goal is customary, not intellectual, and applies to their absolute type, not the kalām-specific instances.

The first observatory of the preliminaries tackles a foundational methodological question: what must every science present before its main content? The commentator clarifies that this obligation is not a strict intellectual necessity but a matter of custom and pedagogical preference. Moreover, the topics required—definition, subject matter, and goal—are universal in their generic sense, not tied to any particular science. This passage establishes the methodological groundwork for the entire work.

Guided Path
1
Understand the type of obligation
The passage distinguishes between intellectual necessity (ʿaqlan) and customary obligation (ʿurfī). Identify the commentator's statement that the obligation is based on deeming it preferable (iʿtibār al-ūlā).
This is key: the topics are not intellectually mandatory but pedagogically recommended.
2
Identify the absolute topics
The commentator says the required topics are the absolute definition (al-taʿrīf), subject matter (al-mawḍūʿ), and goal (al-ghāya), not those specific to kalām. Note how the author's proof in each goal (maqṣad) supports this.
The phrase 'mutlaq al-taʿrīf' etc. appears in the commentary.
3
Analyze the grammatical ellipsis
The passage discusses the grammatical structure of 'its definition' (taʿrīfuhu). The commentator rejects the interpretation that the pronoun refers to kalām, calling that a blunder. Understand why the absolute sense is intended.
The author omitted the word in the six goals to indicate absoluteness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الأول في المقدمات وفيه مراصد ستة ]
المرصد الأول : فيما يجب تقديمه في كل علم ]
وأما المراصد الباقية ففيما يجب تقديمه في هذا العلم كما ستعرفه ولم يرد بوجوب التقديم أنه لا بد منه عقلاً، بل أريد الوجوب العرفي الذي مرجعه اعتبار الأولى
قوله: (فيما يجب تقديمه إلخ أي في بيان ما يجب تقديمه في كل علم يطلب تحصليه وإثبات تقديمه بالدليل، وهي مطلق التعريف والموضوع والغاية وأمثالها لا المخصوصة بالكلام بدليل أنه ذكر المصنف في كل مقصد دليلاً على وجوب تقديم مطلقها، فقول المصنف رحمه الله تعريفه خبر مبتدأ محذوف، أو خبره محذوف أي مما يجب تقديمه تعريفه، أو ما يجب تقديمه تعريفه ولذا ترك كلمة في المقاصد الستة مخالفا لسائر المقاصد والمراصد والمواقف، وقول الشارح رحمه الله : أي تعريف العلم الذي إشارة إلى أن الضمير راجع إلى علم لا إلى كل والتخصيص بالصفة ملحوظ في المرجع بمعونة المقام، وإنما جعل العنوانات في المقاصد الأمور المذكورة مطلقاً، لكونها أهم بالإثبات لأن تقديم الأمور المخصوصة بالكلام إنما وجب لكونها أفراداً لها، ومن قال : إن المراد بها الأمور المخصوصة بالكلام والكلام على حذف المضاف أي تقديم نوعه وأن الضمير في قوله تعريفه راجع إلى الكلام وأن اللام في قول الشارح أي العلم للعهد فقد خبط خبط عشواء .
قوله : ( فيما يجب تقديمه في كل علم اعترض عليه بأن الأمور الموردة ها هنا من التعريف والموضوع وغيرهما هي المضافة إلى علم الكلام فكيف يجب تقديمها في كل علم والجواب : الحمل على حذف المضاف والمعنى ما يجب تقديم نوعه في كل علم شرع في تحصيله وحينئذ يكون ما عبارة عن تلك الأمور المضافة إلى علم الكلام بخصوصها، ويكون الضمير في تعريفه وموضوعه وغيرهما راجعاً إلى خصوصية علم الكلام والعلم في قوله أي تعريف العلم عبارة عنه على أن اللام للعهد ،كالإضافة وإنما لم يقل : أي تعريف علم الكلام إشارة إلى أن الخصوص والإضافة إنما نشأ باعتبار أنه المشروع فيه .
مع
قوله: (ففيما يجب تقديمه في هذا (العلم أي لا في كل علم بقرينة المقابلة، لا أنه لا يجب تقديمه في غير هذا العلم أصلاً كيف وعلم أصول الفقه أيضاً قد صدر بتلك المباحث ؟ واستحسن ذلك، ثم إن مسائل جميع العلوم وإن كانت مرتبطة بها، إلا أن تصدير كتب الصرف مثلاً بها اشتمالها على نوع كثرة، ودقة مما ليس بمستحسن في طرق التعليم قطعاً، وأما تصدير كتب الكلام بها مع أنها جزء منه ففي غاية الاستحسان، فالفرق ظاهر جداً.
The first station (al-mawqif) on the preliminaries, and it contains six observatories (marāṣid). The first observatory: on what must be presented in every science. As for the remaining observatories, they are on what must be presented in this science, as you will know. And by the obligation of presentation is not meant that it is intellectually necessary, but rather the customary obligation whose basis is deeming it preferable.
His statement: 'on what must be presented' etc. i.e., an explanation of what must be presented in every science that one seeks to acquire, and establishing its presentation by proof. These are the absolute definition, subject matter, goal, and the like, not those specific to kalām. The evidence is that the author mentioned in each goal (maqṣad) a proof for the obligation of presenting their absolute forms. Thus the author's statement 'its definition' is a predicate of an omitted subject, or its predicate is omitted, i.e., 'what must be presented is its definition' or 'what must be presented is its definition'. Therefore he omitted the word in the six goals, contrary to the other goals, observatories, and stations. And the commentator's statement: 'i.e., the definition of the science' indicates that the pronoun refers to science, not to 'every', and the specification by the attribute is noted in the referent by context. He only made the headings in the goals the mentioned matters absolutely, because they are more important to prove, since the presentation of the matters specific to kalām is only obligatory because they are instances of them. Whoever says that what is meant is the matters specific to kalām, and that the phrase involves ellipsis of the qualified noun (i.e., presentation of its type), and that the pronoun in 'its definition' refers to kalām, and that the lām in the commentator's statement 'the science' is for definite reference, has blundered badly.
His statement: 'on what must be presented in every science' – an objection was raised against it: the matters mentioned here, such as definition, subject matter, and others, are added to the science of kalām, so how can they be required to be presented in every science? The answer: it is construed as ellipsis of the qualified noun, and the meaning is: what must be presented in every science one begins to acquire is its type; and then the 'what' (mā) refers to those matters added specifically to the science of kalām, and the pronoun in 'its definition', 'its subject matter', and others refers to the specificity of the science of kalām. And 'the science' in his statement 'i.e., the definition of the science' refers to it, with the lām being for definite reference, like a genitive construction. And he did not say 'i.e., the definition of the science of kalām' to indicate that the specificity and genitive relation only arise from the fact that it is the science being embarked upon.
With his statement: 'so in what must be presented in this science' i.e., not in every science, by the context of opposition; not that it should not be presented in other sciences at all. How can that be, when the science of uṣūl al-fiqh also begins with those topics? And that is deemed good. However, the questions of all sciences, even if they are connected to them, the practice of beginning books of morphology (ṣarf) with them, for instance, due to their containing a kind of abundance and precision, is certainly not deemed good in pedagogical methods. But beginning books of kalām with them, since they are a part of it, is in the highest degree of approval. So the difference is very clear.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered commentary on the first observatory (marsad) of the first station (mawqif) on preliminaries. It begins by stating that the first station contains six observatories, and the first observatory concerns 'what must be presented in every science'. It clarifies that the obligation is intellectual but customary (al-wujūb al-‘urfī), based on preference. Then it explains the author's phrase 'fīmā yajibu taqdīmuhu' – it means the absolute definition, subject matter, goal, etc., not those specific to kalām. The commentator addresses the grammatical structure: the author's words are interpreted with ellipsis. Then an objection is raised: how can topics like definition and subject matter, which are added to kalām, be required for every science? The answer is that there is an ellipsis: the 'mā' refers to the type of these topics as applied to the specific science one is beginning. The pronoun in 'its definition' refers to the specific science (kalām). The commentator further distinguishes that the remaining observatories are about what must be presented in this science (kalām) specifically, not every science, but this does not mean other sciences should not begin with such topics – e.g., uṣūl al-fiqh also does. However, the use of these topics in kalām is more appropriate because they are integral to it, whereas in sciences like ṣarf it would be excessive and not pedagogically sound.

Key Terms
مراصد
الوجوب العرفي
التعريف
الموضوع
الغاية
حذف المضاف
المقاصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the pronoun in 'its definition' refers to kalām, and that the required topics are the specific definition, subject, and goal of kalām.
Correction: The pronoun refers to 'science' in general, and the topics are the absolute, generic ones (definition, subject, goal) that apply to any science, not kalām-specific.

Checkpoint
Why did the author omit the word 'muṭlaq' (absolute) in the six goals according to the commentator?
A) Because he wanted to emphasize the kalām-specific topics.
B) Because the absolute sense is understood from context and to indicate absoluteness.
C) Because of a textual error.
D) Because the six goals are less important.

Key Takeaway
The first marsad establishes that every science must begin with a customary presentation of its definition, subject, and goal as generic prerequisites, not as kalām-specific topics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you begin to learn a new Islamic science, do you assume that understanding its definition, subject, and goal is intellectually necessary, or do you treat it as a customary preface? How does this distinction affect the seriousness with which you approach the foundations?
?
The commentator warns against confusing the absolute with the specific, calling such confusion a blunder. In your own pursuit of knowledge, have you ever mistaken a particular teaching or practice for a universal principle? What did you learn from that experience?
?
This lesson teaches that presenting definition, subject, and goal is based on what is 'customary and preferable' (al-ʿurfī alladhī marjiʿuhu iʿtibār al-ūlā). Consider your daily learning habits: are you guided by what is normatively expected, or do you insist on rational necessity? Which approach brings you closer to the reality of the knowledge you seek?

Defending the Phrase: Objection and Answer on 'Every Science'
Commentator addresses an objection and provides a grammatical solution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the apparent contradiction between the universal phrase 'in every science' and the kalām-specific topics mentioned.

Core Idea
The apparent contradiction is resolved by ellipsis: 'in every science' means the type of these topics, not the exact kalām-specific versions.

An objection arises: the topics of definition, subject, and goal presented in this marsad are explicitly tied to kalām, yet the text claims they must be presented in every science. The commentator answers with a grammatical insight: the phrase involves ellipsis of the qualified noun—what must be presented is the type of these topics as applied to whichever science one begins. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for other disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection states: the matters mentioned (definition, subject, goal) are added to (iḍāfat) kalām, so how can they be required for every science? Grasp the logical tension.
The objection assumes the topics are inherently kalām-specific due to the iḍāfa.
2
Grasp the answer: ellipsis
The answer is that the phrase 'in every science' involves ellipsis of the qualified noun (ḥadhf al-muḍāf). The actual meaning is: 'what must be presented is the type of these topics in every science one begins to acquire.' Thus the 'mā' refers to the kalām-specific topics as representative of the type.
This preserves the absolute nature while allowing specific application.
3
Apply to other sciences
The commentator notes that the remaining observatories are about this science (kalām) specifically, but this does not mean other sciences should not present such topics. For example, uṣūl al-fiqh also begins with them. However, it is pedagogically inappropriate in sciences like ṣarf due to abundance and detail.
This shows the principle is flexible and context-sensitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الأول في المقدمات وفيه مراصد ستة ]
المرصد الأول : فيما يجب تقديمه في كل علم ]
وأما المراصد الباقية ففيما يجب تقديمه في هذا العلم كما ستعرفه ولم يرد بوجوب التقديم أنه لا بد منه عقلاً، بل أريد الوجوب العرفي الذي مرجعه اعتبار الأولى
قوله: (فيما يجب تقديمه إلخ أي في بيان ما يجب تقديمه في كل علم يطلب تحصليه وإثبات تقديمه بالدليل، وهي مطلق التعريف والموضوع والغاية وأمثالها لا المخصوصة بالكلام بدليل أنه ذكر المصنف في كل مقصد دليلاً على وجوب تقديم مطلقها، فقول المصنف رحمه الله تعريفه خبر مبتدأ محذوف، أو خبره محذوف أي مما يجب تقديمه تعريفه، أو ما يجب تقديمه تعريفه ولذا ترك كلمة في المقاصد الستة مخالفا لسائر المقاصد والمراصد والمواقف، وقول الشارح رحمه الله : أي تعريف العلم الذي إشارة إلى أن الضمير راجع إلى علم لا إلى كل والتخصيص بالصفة ملحوظ في المرجع بمعونة المقام، وإنما جعل العنوانات في المقاصد الأمور المذكورة مطلقاً، لكونها أهم بالإثبات لأن تقديم الأمور المخصوصة بالكلام إنما وجب لكونها أفراداً لها، ومن قال : إن المراد بها الأمور المخصوصة بالكلام والكلام على حذف المضاف أي تقديم نوعه وأن الضمير في قوله تعريفه راجع إلى الكلام وأن اللام في قول الشارح أي العلم للعهد فقد خبط خبط عشواء .
قوله : ( فيما يجب تقديمه في كل علم اعترض عليه بأن الأمور الموردة ها هنا من التعريف والموضوع وغيرهما هي المضافة إلى علم الكلام فكيف يجب تقديمها في كل علم والجواب : الحمل على حذف المضاف والمعنى ما يجب تقديم نوعه في كل علم شرع في تحصيله وحينئذ يكون ما عبارة عن تلك الأمور المضافة إلى علم الكلام بخصوصها، ويكون الضمير في تعريفه وموضوعه وغيرهما راجعاً إلى خصوصية علم الكلام والعلم في قوله أي تعريف العلم عبارة عنه على أن اللام للعهد ،كالإضافة وإنما لم يقل : أي تعريف علم الكلام إشارة إلى أن الخصوص والإضافة إنما نشأ باعتبار أنه المشروع فيه .
مع
قوله: (ففيما يجب تقديمه في هذا (العلم أي لا في كل علم بقرينة المقابلة، لا أنه لا يجب تقديمه في غير هذا العلم أصلاً كيف وعلم أصول الفقه أيضاً قد صدر بتلك المباحث ؟ واستحسن ذلك، ثم إن مسائل جميع العلوم وإن كانت مرتبطة بها، إلا أن تصدير كتب الصرف مثلاً بها اشتمالها على نوع كثرة، ودقة مما ليس بمستحسن في طرق التعليم قطعاً، وأما تصدير كتب الكلام بها مع أنها جزء منه ففي غاية الاستحسان، فالفرق ظاهر جداً.
The first station (al-mawqif) on the preliminaries, and it contains six observatories (marāṣid). The first observatory: on what must be presented in every science. As for the remaining observatories, they are on what must be presented in this science, as you will know. And by the obligation of presentation is not meant that it is intellectually necessary, but rather the customary obligation whose basis is deeming it preferable.
His statement: 'on what must be presented' etc. i.e., an explanation of what must be presented in every science that one seeks to acquire, and establishing its presentation by proof. These are the absolute definition, subject matter, goal, and the like, not those specific to kalām. The evidence is that the author mentioned in each goal (maqṣad) a proof for the obligation of presenting their absolute forms. Thus the author's statement 'its definition' is a predicate of an omitted subject, or its predicate is omitted, i.e., 'what must be presented is its definition' or 'what must be presented is its definition'. Therefore he omitted the word in the six goals, contrary to the other goals, observatories, and stations. And the commentator's statement: 'i.e., the definition of the science' indicates that the pronoun refers to science, not to 'every', and the specification by the attribute is noted in the referent by context. He only made the headings in the goals the mentioned matters absolutely, because they are more important to prove, since the presentation of the matters specific to kalām is only obligatory because they are instances of them. Whoever says that what is meant is the matters specific to kalām, and that the phrase involves ellipsis of the qualified noun (i.e., presentation of its type), and that the pronoun in 'its definition' refers to kalām, and that the lām in the commentator's statement 'the science' is for definite reference, has blundered badly.
His statement: 'on what must be presented in every science' – an objection was raised against it: the matters mentioned here, such as definition, subject matter, and others, are added to the science of kalām, so how can they be required to be presented in every science? The answer: it is construed as ellipsis of the qualified noun, and the meaning is: what must be presented in every science one begins to acquire is its type; and then the 'what' (mā) refers to those matters added specifically to the science of kalām, and the pronoun in 'its definition', 'its subject matter', and others refers to the specificity of the science of kalām. And 'the science' in his statement 'i.e., the definition of the science' refers to it, with the lām being for definite reference, like a genitive construction. And he did not say 'i.e., the definition of the science of kalām' to indicate that the specificity and genitive relation only arise from the fact that it is the science being embarked upon.
With his statement: 'so in what must be presented in this science' i.e., not in every science, by the context of opposition; not that it should not be presented in other sciences at all. How can that be, when the science of uṣūl al-fiqh also begins with those topics? And that is deemed good. However, the questions of all sciences, even if they are connected to them, the practice of beginning books of morphology (ṣarf) with them, for instance, due to their containing a kind of abundance and precision, is certainly not deemed good in pedagogical methods. But beginning books of kalām with them, since they are a part of it, is in the highest degree of approval. So the difference is very clear.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered commentary on the first observatory (marsad) of the first station (mawqif) on preliminaries. It begins by stating that the first station contains six observatories, and the first observatory concerns 'what must be presented in every science'. It clarifies that the obligation is intellectual but customary (al-wujūb al-‘urfī), based on preference. Then it explains the author's phrase 'fīmā yajibu taqdīmuhu' – it means the absolute definition, subject matter, goal, etc., not those specific to kalām. The commentator addresses the grammatical structure: the author's words are interpreted with ellipsis. Then an objection is raised: how can topics like definition and subject matter, which are added to kalām, be required for every science? The answer is that there is an ellipsis: the 'mā' refers to the type of these topics as applied to the specific science one is beginning. The pronoun in 'its definition' refers to the specific science (kalām). The commentator further distinguishes that the remaining observatories are about what must be presented in this science (kalām) specifically, not every science, but this does not mean other sciences should not begin with such topics – e.g., uṣūl al-fiqh also does. However, the use of these topics in kalām is more appropriate because they are integral to it, whereas in sciences like ṣarf it would be excessive and not pedagogically sound.

Key Terms
حذف المضاف
الوجوب العرفي
التعريف
الموضوع
الغاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the phrase 'in every science' requires every science to present the exact same definition, subject, and goal as kalām.
Correction: The phrase is interpreted with ellipsis: it means the type of these topics (i.e., generic definition, subject, goal) must be presented in every science, adapted to that science.

Checkpoint
What is the key grammatical mechanism used to answer the objection?
A) Emphasis (taʾkīd)
B) Ellipsis of the qualified noun (ḥadhf al-muḍāf)
C) Simile (tashbīh)
D) Metonymy (kināyah)

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction in the phrase 'in every science' is resolved by understanding it as an ellipsis: what must be presented is the generic type of topics, not the kalām-specific instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an apparent contradiction in Islamic texts, do you instinctively seek a deeper grammatical or contextual explanation, or do you feel comfortable dismissing it? This passage shows that even objections founded on careful reading can be resolved with precision. Which temptation—hasty dismissal or neglect—do you fall into, and what will you change to emulate the commentator’s thoroughness?
?
The commentator distinguishes between sciences where preliminaries are commended and those where they are not. Is there an area of your own Islamic learning where you rush into details without establishing foundations? Identify one such area and commit to building a solid base before moving forward.
?
The resolution here hinges on understanding 'type' (nawʿ) to bridge the specific and the general. Are there conflicts in your theological understanding where you have not looked for the underlying ‘type’ that reconciles seemingly opposing specifics? Write down one such conflict and actively research how scholars have applied similar interpretive principles.

The Obligation to Present Preliminaries is Conventional, Not Rational
Clarification of the nature of obligation for presenting preliminaries in every science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation to present preliminaries in every science is based on convention and pedagogical preference rather than rational necessity, and how this affects the structure of theological works.

Core Idea
Presenting preliminaries is a pedagogical convention, not a rational requirement.

Before diving into the content of a science, scholars often present certain preliminary matters such as definitions and the subject matter. But is this obligation a logical necessity? In this passage, the author distinguishes between rational and conventional obligation, clarifying that the presentation of preliminaries is a matter of convention — based on what is preferable for teaching — not an absolute rational requirement. This insight helps us understand the structure of many classical works.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between rational and conventional obligation
Read the opening lines where the author explicitly states that the obligation is not rational but conventional, based on considering what is preferable.
This sets the stage for the entire discussion.
2
Identify what 'what must be presented' refers to
The author clarifies that it refers to absolute matters like definition, subject, and end, which are common to all sciences, not just kalam.
This shows the universal nature of these preliminaries.
3
Examine the pronoun reference discussion
The commentator analyzes the pronoun in 'its definition' and argues it refers to the science itself, not to 'every' or to 'kalam'; this is supported by context.
Understanding pronoun reference prevents misinterpretation.
4
Note the refutation of a mistaken view
The author forcefully rejects the view that the pronoun refers to 'kalam' and that the preliminary matters are specific to kalam, calling it a severe error.
This reinforces the intended interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المراصد الباقية ففيما يجب تقديمه في هذا العلم كما ستعرفه ولم يرد بوجوب التقديم أنه لا بد منه عقلاً، بل أريد الوجوب العرفي الذي مرجعه اعتبار الأولى قوله: (فيما يجب تقديمه إلخ أي في بيان ما يجب تقديمه في كل علم يطلب تحصليه وإثبات تقديمه بالدليل، وهي مطلق التعريف والموضوع والغاية وأمثالها لا المخصوصة بالكلام بدليل أنه ذكر المصنف في كل مقصد دليلاً على وجوب تقديم مطلقها، فقول المصنف رحمه الله تعريفه خبر مبتدأ محذوف، أو خبره محذوف أي مما يجب تقديمه تعريفه، أو ما يجب تقديمه تعريفه ولذا ترك كلمة في المقاصد الستة مخالفا لسائر المقاصد والمراصد والمواقف، وقول الشارح رحمه الله : أي تعريف العلم الذي إشارة إلى أن الضمير راجع إلى علم لا إلى كل والتخصيص بالصفة ملحوظ في المرجع بمعونة المقام، وإنما جعل العنوانات في المقاصد الأمور المذكورة مطلقاً، لكونها أهم بالإثبات لأن تقديم الأمور المخصوصة بالكلام إنما وجب لكونها أفراداً لها، ومن قال : إن المراد بها الأمور المخصوصة بالكلام والكلام على حذف المضاف أي تقديم نوعه وأن الضمير في قوله تعريفه راجع إلى الكلام وأن اللام في قول الشارح أي العلم للعهد فقد خبط خبط عشواء .
As for the remaining observations, they concern what must be presented in this science, as you will come to know. And the intended meaning of the obligation of presentation is not that it is absolutely necessary rationally, but rather the conventional obligation, whose reference is considering what is preferable. His statement (the author's): 'concerning what must be presented, etc.' — that is, a clarification of what must be presented in every science that one seeks to master, and proof of its presentation by evidence, and they are the absolute definition, subject, end, and their likes, not those specific to the science of kalam, as evidence that the author mentioned in each objective a proof for the obligation of presenting the absolute form of them. So the author's statement, 'its definition,' is the predicate of a deleted subject, or its predicate is deleted, i.e., 'what must be presented is its definition,' or 'what must be presented is its definition.' That is why he omitted the word 'in' from the six objectives, contrary to the other objectives, observations, and stations. And the commentator's statement, 'i.e., the definition of the science,' indicates that the pronoun refers to 'science' and not to 'every,' and the specification by the attribute is noted in the referent with the aid of the context. And he only made the titles in the objectives the mentioned matters absolutely, because they are most important to prove, since the presentation of matters specific to the science of kalam is only obligatory because they are individuals of them. And whoever says that the intended meaning is the matters specific to kalam, and that the word 'kalam' is implied, i.e., 'the presentation of its type,' and that the pronoun in 'its definition' refers to 'kalam,' and that the lām in the commentator's statement 'the science' is for denoting previously known reference — such a person has strayed severely.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage begins by clarifying the nature of the obligation to present certain matters at the beginning of every science. The author states that it is not a rational obligation (عقلاً), but rather a conventional obligation (الوجوب العرفي) based on what is considered preferable. Then the commentary explains the phrase 'فيما يجب تقديمه' (what must be presented) by saying it refers to the absolute matters (مطلق التعريف والموضوع والغاية) that are common to every science, not those specific to kalam. The commentator then discusses the pronoun reference in the author's phrase 'تعريفه' (its definition), analyzing whether it refers to the science (علم) or to kalam (الكلام). He concludes that it is better to interpret it as referring to the science, and that the specific sciences are implied through context. He also rejects a mistaken view that takes the pronoun as referring to kalam, calling it confused.

Key Terms
وجوب التقديم
الوجوب العرفي
تعريف
موضوع
غاية
الضمير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that the obligation to present preliminaries in a science is a rational necessity (عقلاً).
Correction: The author explicitly states that it is a conventional obligation (الوجوب العرفي), based on considering what is preferable (اعتبار الأولى), not a rational requirement.

Checkpoint
Why is the obligation to present preliminaries in a science described as conventional (عرفي) rather than rational (عقلي)?
A) Because it is a logical necessity that requires proof
B) Because it is based on pedagogical preference, not intellectual necessity
C) Because the preliminaries are not actually required
D) Because it is a revealed command

Key Takeaway
The obligation to present preliminaries in a science is conventional, based on pedagogical preference, not rational necessity, and the specific matters of kalam are instances of broader categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author firmly refutes a mistaken interpretation, even calling it 'severely strayed'. How do you react when someone corrects your deeply held misinterpretation of a sacred text? Are you quick to accept correction or do you resist? What does your reaction reveal about your attachment to your own understanding?

Objection and Answer: How Can Kalam-Specific Matters Be Required in Every Science?
Objection and answer about presenting specific matters in every science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the objection that matters specific to kalam cannot be required in every science, using the concept of ellipsis (حذف المضاف).

Core Idea
What is required in every science is the type, not the specific instance.

The commentator presents a logical objection: the definition, subject, and purpose discussed are specific to kalam, so how can they be required in every science? The answer involves interpreting the phrase as elliptical, meaning the type of these matters is required. This lesson unpacks that argument and the use of حذف المضاف.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage to understand the objection: the matters (definition, subject) are specific to kalam, so how can they be required in every science?
The objection is based on specificity.
2
Understand the answer via ellipsis
The answer uses حذف المضاف: the phrase means 'the type of these matters' is required. Note how the pronoun 'its definition' refers to kalam.
This resolves the generality.
3
Consider the author's phrasing choice
The commentator explains why the author did not explicitly say 'definition of kalam' – to indicate that the specificity arises from the science under study.
This shows careful reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فيما يجب تقديمه في كل علم اعترض عليه بأن الأمور الموردة ها هنا من التعريف والموضوع وغيرهما هي المضافة إلى علم الكلام فكيف يجب تقديمها في كل علم والجواب : الحمل على حذف المضاف والمعنى ما يجب تقديم نوعه في كل علم شرع في تحصيله وحينئذ يكون ما عبارة عن تلك الأمور المضافة إلى علم الكلام بخصوصها، ويكون الضمير في تعريفه وموضوعه وغيرهما راجعاً إلى خصوصية علم الكلام والعلم في قوله أي تعريف العلم عبارة عنه على أن اللام للعهد ،كالإضافة وإنما لم يقل : أي تعريف علم الكلام إشارة إلى أن الخصوص والإضافة إنما نشأ باعتبار أنه المشروع فيه .
His statement: 'concerning what must be presented in every science' — an objection is raised against it: the matters adduced here, such as definition, subject, and others, are those added to the science of kalam, so how can it be necessary to present them in every science? The answer: by interpreting it as ellipsis of the added term, and the meaning is 'what must be presented is its type in every science that one begins to study.' In that case, 'what' refers to those matters as specifically added to the science of kalam, and the pronoun in 'its definition,' 'its subject,' and others refers to the particularity of the science of kalam, and 'the science' in his statement 'i.e., the definition of the science' refers to it, with the lām denoting previously known reference, like the genitive construction. And he only omitted saying 'i.e., the definition of the science of kalam' to indicate that the particularity and addition arise only from it being the science in which one begins.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection: The matters mentioned here (definition, subject, purpose) are all specific to the science of kalam, so how can the author claim that they must be presented in every science? The answer: by interpreting the phrase 'في كل علم' (in every science) as elliptical, with the implied word 'نوع' (type). The intended meaning is: what must be presented is the type of these matters in every science that one begins to study. So the word 'ما' (what) refers to those matters specifically as they pertain to kalam, but the requirement to present them in every science is by way of their type, not their specific instance. The pronoun in 'تعريفه' (its definition) then refers to the specific science of kalam. The commentator also explains why the author did not explicitly say 'تعريف علم الكلام' (definition of kalam) to indicate that the specificity arises from the context of being the science under study.

Key Terms
حذف المضاف
نوع
اللام للعهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the matters (definition, subject, purpose) are required exactly as they appear in kalam for every science.
Correction: The correct understanding is that only the type of these matters is required in every science; the specific content pertains to kalam.

Checkpoint
How is the objection resolved that the matters (definition, subject, purpose) are specific to kalam but are said to be required in every science?
A) By denying that they are specific to kalam
B) By interpreting 'every science' as 'every science that is similar to kalam'
C) By understanding the phrase as elliptical, meaning the type of these matters is required
D) By saying the obligation is only for kalam

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction is resolved by understanding that the phrase is elliptical: the type of these matters is required in every science, not the specific instance from kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a complex theological argument as a 'technical trick' without understanding the depth of its reasoning? How does this passage challenge you to approach scholarly objections with more humility and care? What will you do differently the next time you encounter a difficult interpretation?
?
This passage shows that general statements often require qualification (ellipsis). In your own study of Islam, where might you be taking general statements from texts too literally without considering implied restrictions? How can you train yourself to recognize such nuances?
?
The author's answer required precise understanding of Arabic grammar. How committed are you to learning the tools (like Arabic, logic) necessary to avoid misunderstanding divine speech? What specific step will you take this week to deepen your study of these foundational sciences?

Why Preliminaries are Presented in Kalam but Not in Every Science
The commentator distinguishes the presentation of preliminaries in kalam from other sciences, using examples from usul al-fiqh and sarf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author specifies that these preliminaries are to be presented in this science (kalam) and not in every science, and the pedagogical reason behind this distinction.

Core Idea
Not all sciences benefit from the same teaching method—kalam's preliminaries are part of its subject matter.

Sanusi has stated that certain matters must be presented at the beginning of this science. But does this apply to all sciences? The commentator clarifies that this is specific to kalam, and he gives a practical example from usul al-fiqh and sarf to show the pedagogical reasoning. This lesson will explore why that distinction matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the commentator's reasoning style.
Pay attention to the contrast between 'this science' and 'every science'.
2
Identify the main claim and its qualification
The commentator first clarifies that the requirement is specific to kalam, but not that it's forbidden in other sciences. He gives the example of usul al-fiqh.
This prevents a misunderstanding that might arise from reading the original text.
3
Compare the examples of sarf and kalam
Notice why sarf is not suitable: its preliminaries contain too much multiplicity and precision. In contrast, kalam's preliminaries are part of its subject matter.
The key phrase is 'مع أنها جزء منه' – because they are part of it.
4
Reflect on the pedagogical principle
Consider the general educational principle: subjects with excessive detail and precision are not good as introductions in some sciences, but in kalam it's appropriate.
This shows that the author is mindful of effective teaching methods.
5
Summarize the difference
The commentator concludes that the difference is very clear between sciences.
This reinforces the point.

مع قوله: (ففيما يجب تقديمه في هذا (العلم أي لا في كل علم بقرينة المقابلة، لا أنه لا يجب تقديمه في غير هذا العلم أصلاً كيف وعلم أصول الفقه أيضاً قد صدر بتلك المباحث ؟ واستحسن ذلك، ثم إن مسائل جميع العلوم وإن كانت مرتبطة بها، إلا أن تصدير كتب الصرف مثلاً بها اشتمالها على نوع كثرة، ودقة مما ليس بمستحسن في طرق التعليم قطعاً، وأما تصدير كتب الكلام بها مع أنها جزء منه ففي غاية الاستحسان، فالفرق ظاهر جداً.

Commentary
This passage discusses the phrase 'في هذا العلم' (in this science) in contrast to 'في كل علم' (in every science). The commentator explains that the obligation here is specific to this science, not that it is not required in other sciences at all. He cites the example of ilm usul al-fiqh, which also begins with such discussions. However, he notes that in sciences like sarf (morphology), presenting such preliminaries is not considered commendable because they contain a lot of detail and precision that is not suitable for the educational approach. But in kalam, since these matters are part of the science itself, it is highly commendable. Thus, the difference is clear.

The Necessity of Defining a Science
Explains why the definition of a science must be presented, with analogy and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of a science must be presented at the outset to provide the student with insight (basira) and avoid blind stumbling.

Core Idea
A proper definition gives holistic insight; without it, study is like riding a blind she-camel.

Sanusi opens his discussion of the six purposes by emphasizing the importance of defining the science of kalam. He argues that a student cannot proceed with real insight unless they first grasp a clear definition, whether a hadd or a rasam. He then illustrates this with a vivid analogy about riding a blind she-camel, warning against starting study without a proper conceptualization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to grasp the main argument: the definition must come first to ensure insight.
Pay attention to the analogy and the objection-response.
2
Identify the two types of definition
Sanusi mentions 'hadd' (limit) and 'rasam' (description). Understand that both provide a holistic grasp.
3
Analyze the analogy of the blind she-camel
See how the author compares conceptualization without definition to riding a blind camel. Notice the rhetorical devices (metaphor, etc.).
The commentator offers two interpretations of 'khabt 'ashwa'.
4
Consider the objection and response
Sanusi anticipates an objection about the necessity of the six preliminaries and answers it, showing the depth of the argument.
5
Reflect on the methodological lesson
Think about how this principle of starting with a definition applies to your own study of kalam or any discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأحق في طرق التعليم) وفيه مقاصد) ستة أيضاً (الأول تعريفه) أي تعريف العلم الذي يطلب تحصيله وإنما وجب تقديم تعريفه ليكون طالبه على بصيرة) في طلبه فإنه إذا تصوره بتعريفه سواء كان حداً لمفهوم اسمه، أو رسماً له، فقد أحاط بجميعه إحاطة إجمالية باعتبار أمر شامل له يضبطه ويميزه عما عداه بخلاف ما إذا تصوره بغيره فإنه وإن فرض أنه يكفيه في طلبه لكنه لا يفيده بصيرةً فيه (فإن من ركب متن العماية بمعنى الباطل ( أوشك أن يخبط خبط عشواء ) وهي الناقة التي لا عمياء) وهي قوله : (بمعنى الباطل) وهو هاهنا التصور بغير التعريف من الوجه الأعم، أو الأخص شبهه بالمركوبة في كون كل منهما سبباً لسلوك طريق الرسول إلى المطلوب، وأثبت المتن والركوب ففي الكلام استعارة بالكناية، وتخييل وترشيح وإنما قال : أوشك لأنه بمجرد التصور المذكور لا الشروع مشتملاً على فوائد الأمور الستة فلا شك أن الشروع بمثل هذه البصيرة موقوف عقلاً على الأشياء الستة، فيكون وجوب تقديمها أيضاً عقلياً والجواب أن توقف الشروع بالبصيرة المخصوصة عقلاً على الأمور المذكورة، إنما يوجب تقديمها على الإطلاق أعني ابتداء من غير تقييد بشيء، إذا كان الشروع بتلك البصيرة واجباً عقلياً على الشارع في العلم، من حيث هو طالب وهذا ظاهر على أنه يمكن أن يقال : المراد مطلق الشروع بالبصيرة، والمراد بتوقفه على الأمور المذكورة توقفه على نوعها كما حققناه في حواشي المطول . يخبط ما لم يشرع في العلم، ثم قول الشارح : وهي الناقة التي إلخ إشارة إلى توجيهين مبنى الأول أن خبط عشواء مصدر للتشبيه والإضافة للاختصاص فيكون تشبيهاً للخبط المعقول بالخبط المحسوس، ومعنى الثاني : أنه مصدر للنوع والإضافة لأدنى ملابسة أي يخبط خبطاً، يراد في قولهم فلان ركب العشواء وهو خبط أمر على غير بصيرة، فافهم فإنه مما زلت فيه الأقدام.
And the most rightful in methods of teaching) and it contains) six purposes as well. (First: its definition) i.e. the definition of the science whose attainment is sought. And the reason why its definition must be presented is so that its student may have insight) in his pursuit. For if he conceptualizes it by its definition, whether it be a limit (hadd) of the concept of its name, or a description (rasam) of it, then he has encompassed its entirety with a holistic grasp by virtue of a comprehensive matter that governs it and distinguishes it from what is other than it, unlike if he conceptualizes it by something else, for even if it is assumed that it suffices him in his pursuit, yet it does not give him insight therein. (For whoever rides the back of blindness in the sense of falsehood) is likely to blunder like the blundering of a blind she-camel) which is the she-camel that is not blind) and it is his saying: (in the sense of falsehood) and it is here conceptualization by other than the definition from the more general aspect, or the more specific. He likened it to the ridden [animal] in that each of them is a cause for traveling the path of the message to the desired goal. And he affirmed the text and the riding, so in the speech there is a metonymical metaphor, and imaginative, and corroboration. And he only said "is likely" because merely the mentioned conceptualization does not [lead to insight?]. The commencement including the benefits of the six matters, there is no doubt that commencement with such insight is rationally dependent on the six things, so the obligation of presenting them is also rational. And the answer: that the dependence of commencement with the specific insight rationally on the mentioned matters only necessitates presenting them absolutely, i.e., from the beginning without restriction by anything, if the commencement with that insight is rationally obligatory upon the one who embarks upon the science, inasmuch as he is a seeker. This is apparent, and it is possible to say: what is meant is absolute commencement with insight, and what is meant by its dependence on the mentioned matters is its dependence on their type, as we have verified in the footnotes of al-Mutawwal. He stumbles as long as he has not embarked upon the science, then the saying of the commentator: "which is the she-camel that" etc. refers to two interpretations: the first is that "khabt 'ashwa'" is a verbal noun for simile and the addition is for specification, so it is a simile of intellectual stumbling to sensory stumbling; the second meaning is that it is a verbal noun for type and the addition is for loose connection, i.e., he stumbles a stumbling intended in their saying "so-and-so rode the blind she-camel" which is stumbling in an affair without insight. So understand, for it is a matter in which feet have slipped.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines the principle of presenting the definition of a science before studying it. The author states that the first purpose is the definition of the science, which must be presented so that the student has insight (basira). There are two types of definition: a hadd (limit) which defines the concept of its name, and a rasam (description) which describes its distinguishing features. Both give a holistic grasp. If one uses something other than a definition (e.g., a more general or more specific concept), it may suffice but does not provide true insight. The author then gives an analogy: one who rides the back of blindness (i.e., relies on a vague notion) is like one who rides a blind she-camel, stumbling without direction. The commentary explains that "blindness" here means conceptualization without a proper definition. The author then addresses an objection: if the definition is necessary for insight, then the six preliminary matters would also be rationally necessary. The answer is that the dependence is conditional on the specific type of insight being obligatory, and that the requirement is only absolute if the insight is rationally obligatory. The passage ends with a note that this has been verified in the footnotes of al-Mutawwal. Then the commentator provides two interpretations of the phrase "khabt 'ashwa'": one as a simile for intellectual stumbling likened to sensory stumbling, and the other as a type of stumbling meaning acting without insight.

رسم
بصيرة
عشواء
خبط

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any vague conceptualization suffices for beginning the study of a science.
Correction: Sanusi argues that only a proper definition (hadd or rasam) provides the necessary holistic insight; otherwise, one is like a rider of a blind she-camel, stumbling without direction.

Why Every Science Needs a Subject Matter
Sanusi explains why every science needs a subject matter for distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the subject matter of a science is what distinguishes it from other sciences and enables effective teaching.

Core Idea
The subject matter is the identity of a science.

This passage from al-Sanusi explains a foundational idea in Islamic epistemology: that sciences are distinguished not by their goals or methods, but by their subject matters. Before diving into kalam itself, we must understand this organizing principle that governs all disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence – this is because the source text is truncated.
2
Identify the reason for needing a subject matter
The passage states that presenting the subject matter gives the desired science additional distinction (mazid imtiaz). Ask yourself: why does a science need distinction?
The answer is in the next sentence: because sciences are distinguished in themselves through their subject matters.
3
Understand the human cognitive context
Sanusi explains that human perfection lies in knowing realities, but these are numerous and scattered. Effective teaching requires organizing knowledge into distinct sciences.
This is the rationale for why subject matters are important – they bring order.
4
Reflect on how subject matters are defined
The ancients grouped essential states and accidents (ahwal wa a'rad dhatiyya) related to one thing absolutely, from one aspect, or to proportionate things.
Think of examples: arithmetic deals with numbers absolutely, physics with bodies as changeable, geometry with shapes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : موضوع العلم الذي يراد تحصيله، وإنما وجب تقديم موضوعه أي التصديق بموضوعيته، ليمتاز العلم المطلوب عند الطالب مزيد امتياز ( إذ به ) أي بالموضوع ( تتمايز (العلوم في أنفسها وبيان ذلك أن كمال النفس الإنسانية في قوتها الإدراكية إنما هو بمعرفة حقائق الأشياء وأحوالها بقدر الطاقة البشرية، ولما كانت تلك الحقائق وأحوالها متكثرة متنوعة وكانت معرفتها مختلطةً منتشرة متعسرة وغير مستحسنة اقتضى حسن التعليم وتسهيله أن تجعل مضبوطة متمايزة فتصدى لذلك الأوائل فسموا الأحوال والأعراض الذاتية المتعلقة بشيء واحد إما مطلقاً، أو من جهة واحدة أو بأشياء متناسبة تناسباً معتداً به
The second topic: the subject matter of the science that is intended to be acquired. And it was necessary to present its subject matter, i.e., to affirm its being the subject matter, so that the desired science may be distinguished for the student with additional distinction. For by it (i.e., by the subject matter) the sciences are distinguished in themselves. The explanation of that is that the perfection of the human soul in its cognitive power lies in knowing the realities of things and their states to the extent of human capacity. And since those realities and their states are numerous and varied, and their knowledge is mixed, scattered, difficult, and not deemed good, the good of teaching and its facilitation demanded that they be made fixed and distinct. So the ancients undertook that, and they called the essential states and accidents related to one thing, either absolutely, or from one aspect, or to things proportionate with a considered proportion.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage introduces the second topic (al-maqsad al-thani): the subject matter of a science. It argues that presenting the subject matter is necessary for distinguishing sciences clearly. The reason is that human perfection lies in knowing the realities of things, but these realities are numerous and varied, making scattered knowledge difficult. Therefore, for effective teaching, knowledge must be organized into distinct sciences. The ancients achieved this by grouping essential states and accidents (al-ahwal wa al-a'rad al-dhatiyya) that relate to one thing—either absolutely (e.g., number for arithmetic), or from a specific aspect (e.g., body as subject to change for physics), or to multiple things that share a proportional relation (e.g., line, surface, and solid in geometry). The passage ends mid-sentence at the page break, but the idea is that these groupings form the basis for distinguishing sciences.

تمييز العلوم
عرض ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The subject matter of a science is the same as its definition or purpose.
Correction: The subject matter is what the science studies, not its definition (which distinguishes it from other sciences) nor its purpose (which is the goal of studying it). The passage emphasizes that the subject matter is the basis for distinction, not the definition itself.

Key Takeaway

The Conventional Basis for Distinguishing Sciences
The author explains how sciences are distinguished by their subject matter and that this is conventional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the classification of sciences by their subject matter is a conventional practice, not a rational necessity, and that the subject primarily distinguishes objects of knowledge, not the sciences themselves.

Core Idea
Science classification is conventional, not necessary.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a fundamental methodological question: How are sciences distinguished? The author explains that the later scholars followed a certain method, but it is not rationally required. This opens the door to understanding that the grouping of questions under a science is a matter of convention. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's flow come through before interpreting.
Notice the phrase 'amr istihsānī' – a commendable matter.
2
Identify the central claim
The author states there is no rational impediment to treating each question as its own science or grouping many questions together. This establishes the conventional nature of science classification.
Think: why would someone think classification is necessary? The author refutes that.
3
Understand the nuance about subject
The distinction gained through the subject primarily belongs to the objects of knowledge, secondarily to the sciences. Also, the subject may or may not be included in definitions depending on what is being defined.
This clarifies that the subject is not an essential differentiator for the science itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الأول - المرصد الأول: فيما يجب تقديمه في كل علم. سلكت الأواخر هذه الطريقة في علومهم، وهو أمر استحساني إذ لا مانع عقلا من أن تعد كل مسألة علماً برأسه، وتفرد بالتعليم، ولا من أن تعد مسائل كثيرة غير متشاركة في موضوع واحد، سواء كانت متناسبة من وجه آخر أو لا، علماً واحداً، وتفرد بالتدوين. واعلم أن الامتياز الحاصل للطالب بالموضوع إنما هو للمعلومات بالأصالة، وللعلوم بالتبع. والحاصل بالتعريف على عكس ذلك، إن كان تعريفاً للعلم، وأما إن كان تعريفاً للمعلوم فالفرق أنه قد لا يلاحظ الموضوع في التعريف.
The First Position - The First Observation Point: On what must be presented in every science. The later scholars followed this method in their sciences, and it is a commendable matter, for there is no rational impediment to considering each question a science in itself and teaching it separately, nor to considering many questions that do not share one subject—whether they are proportionate from another aspect or not—as one science and recording it separately. Know that the distinction acquired by the student through the subject belongs primarily to the objects of knowledge, and to the sciences secondarily. What is obtained by definition is the opposite: if it is a definition of the science, otherwise if it is a definition of the known, the difference is that the subject may not be considered in the definition.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of scientific classification. The author states that the practice of grouping questions under a single science based on a common subject is a matter of convention (istihsān), not rational necessity. There is no rational objection to treating each question as an independent science, nor to grouping many unrelated questions together. The key point is that the subject matter primarily distinguishes the objects of knowledge, and secondarily the sciences themselves. When defining a science, the subject is usually mentioned; but when defining the known, the subject may be omitted, as in the definition of kalām if taken as a definition of its object.

Key Terms
موضوع
علم
استحساني
تعريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sciences are necessarily divided by their subject matters.
Correction: It is a conventional practice, not a rational necessity. There is no logical bar against alternative classifications.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the basis for distinguishing one science from another?
A) A logical necessity imposed by the nature of knowledge
B) A conventional or commendable practice
C) The number of questions in each science
D) The usefulness of the science

Key Takeaway
The division of sciences by subject is a commendable convention, not an intellectual necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How will you respond to this freedom—will it make you more intentional about why you study what you study, or risk making you less serious?
?
When you learn a new subject, do you primarily focus on its subject matter or its definition? The author notes that the distinction is 'primarily for objects of knowledge, secondarily for sciences.' Which are you actually seeking—knowledge of the object, or mastery of the science itself? Be honest: does your study aim more at knowing God (the object) or at mastering the science of kalām?
?
The passage says there is 'no rational impediment' to grouping unrelated questions into one science. Is there any area in your own learning where you have rigidly separated topics that could be integrated? What would change if you allowed yourself to group them differently, following this conventional principle?
